Is anything done to replicate the Tang-Song transition/evolution? The gameplay of houses is more for Tang Dynasty where aristocratic families dominated government posts. The Khmer Empire shouldn't be called "Angkor". That's a modern term. The capital was actually called Yasodharapura during the empire, Angkor is a modern term. The empire referred to itself as Kambuja or Kambujadeśa.

## Reply 55 — participant_046 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399416

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Where the Byzantine governors are solely appointed based on spending Influence however, the Chinese system relies more closely on Merit. Merit is a value that you build up during a lifetime in public service, through serving in important jobs and taking good decisions. Merit cannot be spent like a currency, instead it is how you are compared to other applicants for any position. A high merit score might mean you can become a circuit (kingdom) tier governor, or even a minister in the central government. Merit does not replace Influence since Influence can still be earned and spent for more underhanded uses, even in a Celestial realm. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
In what way does central administration at the court/imperial capital exist and work for Chinese celestial government? By that I mean people who aren't governors, but ministers/sub-minsters and people in various departments. It's stated here that you can become a minister in central government. But I guess you cannot be both a governor and a minister in central government at the same time?

## Reply 56 — participant_019 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399417

## Reply 57 — participant_042 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399421

What’s done to replicate the idea that some military governors in the Tang Dynasty in 867 are de facto independent from the empire like the Hebei Three Garrisons? Also if something is done to simulate the unruly soldiers of the period. Most of the time the Tang Dynasty’s chief opponent wasn’t the actual military governors, but the soldiers under them. The soldiers regularly kill their military governors. Whenever the Tang government asserted control over a military governorate and appointed their own man, the soldiers kill/expel them and reassert their independence. Even the unruly military governors themselves often had to suck up to the demands of these soldiers or risk getting replaced/killed. This meant that they often tried to seek recognition of the Tang court to try to gain some kind of legitimacy even when they are de facto independent.

## Reply 71 — participant_055 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399452

Hello, I'm the China researcher for the Rajas of Asia and the Fallen Eagle mods. We've been discussing the impending AUH update since it was announced, and I have some questions and comments about this post. What is intended by the split between the Khitan and Liao in the north? That seems ahistorical, but maybe there's some kind of planned representation for the dual nomadic-admin system up north? This is one thing I was very worried about when I saw the initial announcement, but how much of China's Dynastic Cycle is based on specific historical trends? In 867, the Tang Dynasty's central army had been taken over by eunuchs and nepotism, while most of the northeast had drifted into de facto warlord independence, and Uyghur & Shatuo Turks became necessary as mercenaries to even fight wars. This is a very different set of problems to the one that would face, say, Southern Song (which I assume would also be in the unstable phase?), because the central military was purposefully weakened as a matter of government policy (the wu/wei split, a reaction to Tang warlordism), and the Song was at times effectively a tributary to the Xia and Liao through the Chanyuan Covenant. My concern here is that the Dynastic Cycle mechanic might be too abstract to represent the specificities of those different situations. Will the treasury mechanism for China affect Byzantium at all? A friend of mine, the Oxford Byzantinist who also consults for The Fallen Eagle, has been pointing to that sort of centralized treasury with salaries as a much more accurate model for Byzantine governance. The Chinese southwest, from roughly western Guandong to western Sichuan, should not be part of the Song tag. It would be small tusi polities of borderland cultures, the ancestors of today's Zhuang, Qiang, Yao, etc speaking ethnic minorities. The most famous of these is probably the Chiefdom fo Bozhou. This is coming secondhand from an Indonesian friend, but the interior of Borneo should not be wasteland. That wasn't as densely urbanized as parts of the coast, sure, but there were still many people and polities in the interior!

This dev diary is awesome! I am super excited for it. 3(and a half, with Japan double dealing) governments is crazy. Absolutely loving this, and I especially love the Treasury system. Being paid a salary rather than just skimming off the top is great. And I am sure that there will be plenty of oppurtunity to skim from your governance's treasury regardless. Some general questions I hope are able to be answered: 1. Will Korea get a Celestial government? Or a modified variant of one? 2. Will China be able to come out of an era of chaos into different empires, like a Northern and Southern dynasties period? Or will it always try and unify. 3. Will Liao and Jin also be considered Chinese Emperors? Will they have unique content and access to Chinese style gameplay? ## Reply 88 — participant_065 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399506

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Trin Tragula said: Hello and welcome to this first development diary about the upcoming All Under Heaven DLC, and its associated update! I am Trin Tragula, one of the design leads on Crusader Kings III and today I get to talk about East Asia in the middle ages! This diary is going to be a high level one, where we look at the vision for the entire expansion and all we aim to do in it. It will be followed by many more detailed diaries for the features and the individual regions of the expansion. View attachment 1303500 [A first look at the world of Crusader Kings with everything between the Atlantic and the Pacific Ocean. Like everything else in this diary the map is work in progress and subject to change; this includes coloring of the map itself, which is something we are experimenting a bit with right now. Don’t hesitate to tell us what you think of it!] Apart from being Grand Strategy Games, the Paradox games have, for me personally, always also been a way to discover the entire world in a given time period, something that is both a game and a travel guide to the past. With this update, CK will finally get rid of the last artificial barriers to that kind of experience, as the map will no longer arbitrarily end in Burma and Tibet. This lets us shine a spotlight on some of the most interesting states of the time period, such as Tang and Song China, Heian Japan, Angkor, and many others. With All Under Heaven, we want to not just add the missing parts of the map but also fill it with the living world of the past. The goal is that the new part of the map should feel like an integral part of the game. This means new governments with their own features, but it also means things like special buildings, new cultural traditions, and visual variety. East Asia will have its own look, with event illustrations, clothes, ethnicities, holdings, throne rooms, artifacts, and much more. View attachment 1303501 As a final word of this introduction: this is also a work in progress. This is a vision and an overview of what we are making, but development is an iterative process and so this diary is also an opportunity for you to give your input on the things we are looking to add. More details are also forthcoming for each and every thing here in the coming diaries but there is nothing stopping you from telling us already if there are things you think we should include or change. This goes for art, features, and even the map itself. China​A central focus of an East Asian expansion, by virtue of its sheer size as well as the reach and impact of its influence and culture, has to be China. While it was in a very different situation in 867, 1066 and 1178 respectively, China remains both a state that others emulated and a political constant in all of these dates. We will talk a lot more about China and its features in future diaries, but here is a short overview of what we have planned and how we see its purpose. Hegemony - A New Title Tier​Since China is a de jure realm that is more expansive than any one of the empires we currently have in the game, we are adding a new title tier for it - a Hegemony. In all our start dates, China is the only existing Hegemony, but others can be created using the decisions we have for uniting India or Rome. For now, there is no generic way to create these super-empires, and beyond China there are no de-jure ones. Instead, we reserve its use for special cases with bespoke rules and justifications. View attachment 1303502 Celestial Government & Merit​China was a bureaucratic state, and though it differed a bit depending on era, positions in government as well as in provincial administration were largely given to career officials based on their perceived merit. Thousands would take the imperial examinations in order to start their career in public service, leading to possibly the most well-educated government apparatus in the world. In All Under Heaven, the Hegemony of China has a special type of government called the Celestial Government. It builds on the Administrative Government introduced in Roads to Power for the Byzantine Empire. House heads and governors are playable, and can build up Influence and acquire positions, while families have a domicile representing their permanent powerbase that they can build up over time. Where the Byzantine governors are solely appointed based on spending Influence however, the Chinese system relies more closely on Merit. Merit is a value that you build up during a lifetime in public service, through serving in important jobs and taking good decisions. Merit cannot be spent like a currency, instead it is how you are compared to other applicants for any position. A high merit score might mean you can become a circuit (kingdom) tier governor, or even a minister in the central government. Merit does not replace Influence since Influence can still be earned and spent for more underhanded uses, even in a Celestial realm. View attachment 1303503 In order to ensure a healthy crop of potential administrators at all times, the Empire will regularly host examination activities where the applicants can earn a base merit score (or even a higher one) by demonstrating their skills at the Confucian classics, literary style, and essay writing. The Emperor, on the other hand, can control what skills are prioritized in the examinations by controlling the curriculum when hosting Exams. Whatever qualities are emphasized will shape which characters are likely to be able to become governors in the future. The Imperial Treasury​Governors in China will not support themselves using the money from the region they govern. Instead they will send the funds upwards, to the Emperor and his central treasury. The emperor then pays out a salary (gold) to the governor but also assigns funds to the respective treasury of all governorships in the realm. Only gold can be spent on personal expenses, such as building up your family estate, paying for tutorship for your children, and so on. Treasury is what is used for any expenses related to governing. This includes things like building buildings, paying troops, or disaster relief. Like with everything in this diary, there will be more information about the treasury, including details around how money can be put into or taken out of it by characters, in a future China-centric Developer Diary. The Dynastic Cycle​Chinese history is traditionally ordered by the dynasty currently in power. This is a phenomenon that goes far back in time, where often a new dynasty would prioritize writing the history of their predecessors. They would use the imperial archives to chart out and describe how the old dynasty rose, how they ruled and how they eventually lost their way and succumbed to corruption (leaving an opening for the new dynasty to save the day and restore order and propriety). In our timeline, the Tang, once a very successful and expansive dynasty that ruled a vast and prosperous realm, are already troubled by 867. About 40 years from this start date, their dynasty would break into a multi-factional civil war known as the Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms Era. Eventually the Song took power and built a new dynasty based even more strongly on the principles of bureaucracy and internal development. The Song were eventually replaced by the Yuan dynasty of Kublai Khan, who ruled a China that was unmistakably Chinese, but also still very Mongol dominated. The Yuan and other foreign dynasties are often referred to as Conquest Dynasties due to their different nature (at least initially). View attachment 1303504 In All Under Heaven, we have taken inspiration from how the Chinese described this at the time, a Dynastic Cycle where China can be in a stable era, essentially a type of golden age, where it can build its wealth and progress orienting themselves outwards (like the Tang), focusing on military expansion and a vast tributary network. Another focus can be advancing the core region, with great building projects, economic development, innovation and a strong bureaucracy (more like the Song or later Ming). Common to both types of Stable eras (Expansion and Advancement) is that they can go on for a very long time. As long as the rulers put the right people in positions of government, protect their subjects from invasions and act swiftly against natural disasters, they are able to draw on the resources of China to perform great things. The eras come with different possibilities and rules for how to achieve these goals. In this way China’s neighbors will also know what to expect from the neighboring behemoth. Common to both stable eras is that how Legitimacy is built and maintained relates to you moving in alignment with the era. If your dynasty has chosen an Expansion era then your Legitimacy will suffer if you fail to expand and will be even more damaged if outsiders take land or raid your lands. With the ruling dynasty faltering, the cycle will eventually proceed to the Unstable Era. This is an era with its own rules and impact, just like the two stable variants, but here it is mainly to signify a period of change. Here people have begun to question the mandate of the rulers, and every action they take is under closer scrutiny. Natural disasters, wars, and other things will be viewed in a much more negative light, especially if the central government is unable to act quickly on them. Governors will be less selfless and may not take their responsibilities to the center as seriously, instead preparing for their own future. Should the Emperor’s Legitimacy be impacted strongly enough, this era is very likely to slide into a Chaotic Era, where the ruling dynasty will fracture into a multi-factional civil war, much like what happened in the Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms Era. Should the ruling dynasty, on the other hand, navigate the unstable era well enough and emerge on the other side, then they will once again enter a stable era and be able to choose between Advancement and Expansion once more. In the 867 bookmark the Tang starts in the unstable era. In the Chaotic Era, the Hegemony of China title is suspended while the various empires, kingdoms and duchies in the Chinese realm are splintered into separate realms. These will quickly try to form their own dynasties, in conflict with each other. Once a winner emerges China will once again enter a stable phase, and the new ruling dynasty will be seen to have the Mandate of Heaven to rule the restored Hegemony of China. This will mean choosing a type of stable era as well as taking some other decisions about your long term goals (including what capital to use and the traditional name and color to associate with your dynasty). There is much more to be said about the Dynastic Cycle, including how your bureaucrats coordinate to push for trying to change the current type of era, and how it deals with foreign conquest dynasties, but most of that will have to wait for a future diary of its own. Tributaries and China​For the Chinese Empire, tributaries are not at all what they are to the rulers in the steppe. Independent rulers can choose to enter a relationship with China and be legitimized by trappings of authority from China. The tributary is not expected to play any part in China’s wars, and China will also not defend its tributaries as a general rule, it will however be less likely to be attacked directly. Tributaries can attempt to create a closer relationship with their Suzerain Hegemon by going on Tributary missions to the Chinese court, bringing gifts, goods, eunuchs, and other things to please the Emperor. In return they may receive economic benefits, legitimizing artifacts or progress towards Chinese innovations. Japan​One of the more unique regions of the medieval world is Japan. In our timeline (covering 867-1453 AD), Japan is originally a state that is largely inspired by the Chinese way of doing things, with governors who do not directly own land, and a vibrant capital in Kyoto. Noble families play a bigger part in ceremony, fine arts, and intrigue than they do in China, but military campaigns tend to be small and directed at the Ainu or Emishi in the eastern part of the island rather than other families in the realm. Over time, some nobles manage to install themselves in hereditary fiefs, and the country starts undergoing a gradual militarization, culminating in the establishment of something much closer to the feudal realms of the west. Soryō and Ritsuryō - Two Types of Vassals in One Realm​Unlike Administrative realms, where governorships are generally duchies, (and unlike China where they can be any tier) Japanese governorships are generally counties. This means power is a lot more fragmented than it is in other realms. In 867 and 1066, the vast majority of counties are held by Ritsuryō rulers - a government very similar to Administrative - while by 1178 most of Japan is ruled by local Soryō lords - more akin to Feudal. Ritsuryō rulers use Influence to secure appointments and generally don’t have strong armies (unless enabled by their House-related bonuses as described below). Soryō vassals, on the other hand, have gained de facto control of a region from the central state and made it a permanent fief. Their succession is hereditary, their armies are stronger, and they can more easily expand militarily to control multiple counties. Soryō vassals can be useful to the empire in times of war, but generally they are a liability and a Ritsuryō that tries to turn their governorship into a Soryō fief will be considered a criminal. House Relations & Blocs​The long-term relationships between different Houses are now tracked, and move along a sliding scale from great friendship to intense rivalry. Whenever a member of a House takes a significant friendly or hostile action against another House’s member, such as a marriage or a murder, the relation between the Houses will change to reflect the act. View attachment 1303505 In Japan, houses can also form Blocs. These are groups of houses that all follow the head of one house, largely based on house relations. A bloc is a military alliance that is represented on the map, similar to steppe confederations, though with a clear leader and more far-reaching responsibilities. Bloc members will join the factions and wars of their leader (offensive as well as defensive). This means that when wars break out in Japan they are not as limited despite the fragmented political situation. The Emperor and the Kampaku​In our era, the Japanese Emperors had varying degrees of power, but were generally relegated to a more ceremonial role than many of their foreign counterparts. The de facto ruler of Japan in all but name is usually the Kampaku, a Ritsuryō bureaucrat who rules on the Emperor’s behalf. The succession of the Kampaku is determined in a similar way to the Byzantine Emperor’s acclamation, but with a different set of weights (favoring among other things marriage ties with the imperial family). The Emperor, or Ten’nō, still has great ceremonial importance and is playable, but only directly rules his own family manor in all our start dates. Like many of the noble families in Japan, he resides in the imperial capital of Kyoto. View attachment 1303506 Only Ritsuryō rulers may become Kampaku by appointment, but Soryō rulers can take the top job if they can build a powerful enough Faction, and thus establish themselves as a Shōgun (the Soryō equivalent of a Kampaku) by force. The Emperor themselves can also aspire to the top position and, if appointed as Kampaku, is given the option to abdicate the imperial throne and rule as a cloistered emperor, representing periods of strong royal influence. Since the Kampaku, or Shōgun, essentially acts as the top liege in the Japanese realm, they wield an enormous amount of power, and will also make use of special Imperial Policy laws to control what can and cannot be done by vassals in the realm. Should a Shōgunate be established by a Soryō ruler this will start to usher in a societal change with more and more of the vassals in Japan forming Soryō fiefs. Japanese Houses​With most vassals in Japan starting out holding just one county, there is a great need for other ways to build permanent power. The family manor, which is available to both Soryō and Ritsuryō rulers, is one way to build up durable power, and Japanese rulers will have many unique manor buildings for this purpose. Costs and benefits are both modified to better fit the needs and resources available in Japan. For the Soryō especially, this is how special men-at-arms are strengthened and unlocked. Another way to build up the power of your house is to invest your prestige into House Aspirations. House aspirations have a theme and spending more prestige to upgrade them will unlock abilities and modifiers that will allow you to both specialize and grow the resources available to you. Aspirations will allow you to shape the strengths of your House by focusing on military might, intrigue, or economic development, and will also unlock certain upgrades for your family manor. Southeast Asia​View attachment 1303507 Southeast Asia in this timeline is not yet as close to the Chinese sphere of influence as Japan or Korea. The mainland as well as the islands are a mix of indigenous faiths with tribal rulers and Indophile states practicing Hinduism or Buddhism. Unlike the bureaucratic nature of the realms to their north, the realms here are often more transitory in nature. Small central realms often control networks of tributaries that contract or grow with relatively little drama. The Great Empires of this region were often more advanced versions of this, with priests administering the realm on behalf of self proclaimed God Kings based in enormous temple cities. Mandala Government​In All Under Heaven, Buddhist and Hindu states in southeast Asia will predominantly start with the new Mandala Government. This government form initially has a lower vassal and domain limit, and instead relies on its own flavor of tributaries in a greater way. Mandala realms have the new Temple Citadel as their main holding type, though they can also hold castles and temples. Devaraja - God Kings​An independent Mandala ruler is considered a God King, or Devaraja. Most of your authority and power will stem from piety and many things that would otherwise cost prestige or even gold will now consume piety. To be a Devaraja means aspiring to be an ideal ruler in Buddhism or Hinduism, with your radiance attracting tributaries and vassals to your cause. The more convincing you are in this respect the more tributaries you will be able to steal from other rulers, and the more your power can grow. Devarajas also have access to several new levels of devotion, with increasing benefits that will increase the initially restrictive domain and vassal limits of the government. By advancing your status as a God King, a Mandala can eventually end up quite powerful, even if the initial drawbacks in the form of lower vassal and domain limits might make the government seem weak at first glance. Capital Temple​View attachment 1303509 As a God King, the cult around your person is essentially the same thing as your authority as a ruler. To remind everyone of their power, rulers would construct great temple cities. Complexes such as Angkor Wat or Borobudur still stand today and demonstrated the divine power of rulers by showcasing their power and by providing a ceremonial center for the realms of the god kings. Capital Temples make use of the new Great Project feature to let you, your vassals, and your tributaries invest in the growth of your Capital Temple. While they are costly, Capital Temples are the first way in which you can alleviate the domain and vassal limit restrictions of your government type along with many other bonuses. A grand capital temple is also what will make tributaries remain with your heirs, who may not yet be as impressive in their role as divine rulers as you are. Devaraja Aspects​Similar to the Japanese House Aspirations, as a God King you will be able to invest piety into developing your personal cult. Focusing on warlike qualities will make it easier to gain piety through wars. Focusing on serenity might instead mean you can earn piety through more peaceful means, as an example. Your choice of Devaraja aspects will also decide what benefits you get from having high levels of devotion. Additional Features in All Under Heaven​Great Projects​As mentioned above, another new feature to this update is Great Projects. This is a way for multiple rulers to pool their resources together to create something. Usually, this would be the vassals in a realm coming together to help fund something like the Great Wall, but it can also be local governors banding together to help push through disaster relief after a Natural Disaster. Benefits can be both direct, related to what the goal of the project itself is, and more individual, in terms of what the contributors get for helping. A new type of building slot has also been introduced to allow Great Projects to create their own type of buildings that coexists with Special Buildings or Monuments. Not all Great Projects relate to a great building or monument however, though all will have tangible effects once they are completed. There are a number of new Great Projects introduced in All Under Heaven. Like with all other features described today, we will talk more about what projects exist and about Great Projects as a feature in a future diary. Natural Disasters​Natural Disasters are a type of recurring Situation that can strike in known areas. While the disaster impact is over relatively quickly, the Situation will linger and center on the rebuilding of the region after something like a great flood. Every Natural disaster comes with a related Great Project, which means multiple characters in the same area can come together to fund the recovery effort. To this end, the projects also come with a number of optional investments that can potentially leverage the situation either for political benefits or lead to rebuilding the area to be more prosperous than it was prior to the disaster. Our intent here is not that natural disasters be a random and extremely punishing blow to your game but rather something to overcome that can present you with opportunities to demonstrate your skills as a ruler and use it as a possibility to ultimately advance your goals. The Silk Road​While China was enormously important in East Asia and its surroundings, for many parts of the world China’s impact was more indirect. We wanted to take the opportunity to include this in the expansion, since it was still quite tangible. For millennia, goods and innovations have flowed from China towards the west. Trade is not a feature of All Under Heaven, but the Silk Road will be visible on the map and controlling its entrepots will yield economic benefits if China is in an era where it is stable. Another venue that will be open to western rulers is that travelers to the entrepots of the Silk Road may help spread Chinese innovations to their home realms. What About the Other Parts of the Map?​This diary tries to give an overview of all major features for the expansion, but it also leaves a lot unsaid for future diaries. In the coming weeks, we will be giving a closer overview of not only the features already described but also things not mentioned, such as Korea, the northern steppe, the tribal states in southeast Asia, and, of course, the map itself. Next week’s diary will start out by giving an overview of the new map, which will then be followed by more detailed breakdowns as we go through the features for each area. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
As China expands, I hoped that a new setting will be added to the game: the Duhufu. Historically, after the Chinese dynasties expanded their territories, they often established Duhufus as secondary administrative units under the central government, primarily responsible for military defense. For instance, during internal conflicts in the Tang Dynasty, several Duhufu troops were mobilized to quell the rebellion. When fighting abroad, the armies of the Duhufus were frequently deployed to assist the central army, which was a key strategy for the Central Plains dynasties to control vast border regions and diverse cultures.

## Reply 90 — participant_067 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399511

> darthfanta said: What’s done to replicate the idea that some military governors in the Tang Dynasty in 867 are de facto independent from the empire like the Hebei Three Garrisons? Also if something is done to simulate the unruly soldiers of the period. Most of the time the Tang Dynasty’s chief opponent wasn’t the actual military governors, but the soldiers under them. The soldiers regularly kill their military governors. Whenever the Tang government asserted control over a military governorate and appointed their own man, the soldiers kill/expel them and reassert their independence. Even the unruly military governors themselves often had to suck up to the demands of these soldiers or risk getting replaced/killed. This meant that they often tried to seek recognition of the Tang court to try to gain some kind of legitimacy even when they are de facto independent. Click to expand...

## Reply 92 — participant_042 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399522

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Dystopianist said: Hello, I'm the China researcher for the Rajas of Asia and the Fallen Eagle mods. We've been discussing the impending AUH update since it was announced, and I have some questions and comments about this post. What is intended by the split between the Khitan and Liao in the north? That seems ahistorical, but maybe there's some kind of planned representation for the dual nomadic-admin system up north? This is one thing I was very worried about when I saw the initial announcement, but how much of China's Dynastic Cycle is based on specific historical trends? In 867, the Tang Dynasty's central army had been taken over by eunuchs and nepotism, while most of the northeast had drifted into de facto warlord independence, and Uyghur & Shatuo Turks became necessary as mercenaries to even fight wars. This is a very different set of problems to the one that would face, say, Southern Song (which I assume would also be in the unstable phase?), because the central military was purposefully weakened as a matter of government policy (the wu/wei split, a reaction to Tang warlordism), and the Song was at times effectively a tributary to the Xia and Liao through the Chanyuan Covenant. My concern here is that the Dynastic Cycle mechanic might be too abstract to represent the specificities of those different situations. Will the treasury mechanism for China affect Byzantium at all? A friend of mine, the Oxford Byzantinist who also consults for The Fallen Eagle, has been pointing to that sort of centralized treasury with salaries as a much more accurate model for Byzantine governance. The Chinese southwest, from roughly western Guandong to western Sichuan, should not be part of the Song tag. It would be small tusi polities of borderland cultures, the ancestors of today's Zhuang, Qiang, Yao, etc speaking ethnic minorities. The most famous of these is probably the Chiefdom fo Bozhou. This is coming secondhand from an Indonesian friend, but the interior of Borneo should not be wasteland. That wasn't as densely urbanized as parts of the coast, sure, but there were still many people and polities in the interior! Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I think they need to simulate overcentralization and decentralization, and then also the status of soldiers vs Confucian scholars somehow. Much of the weakness of the Northern Song Dynasty had to do with overcentralization. The creation of a military force that concentrates mainly around the capital while the weakest troops were located around the borders to prevent them from causing trouble. This caused problems when the central military force got corrupted by the luxuries of the capital due to perpetual lack of combat and the soldiers often taking other jobs instead of training. This was in direct response to the unruliness of the regional armies of the Tang Dynasty, where soldiers outside the earshot of the emperor caused all sorts of mayhem.At the same time this also meant that the border armies aren’t really adequately resourced to fight border wars.

Request for Game Features Related to Japanese Historical and Religious Themes Heretical Shinto Sects I would like to see heretical forms of Shinto-based faiths implemented in the game, such as beliefs similar to the Tokoyo-no-Kami These would be deviations from orthodox Shinto, and could represent underground or suppressed sects with mystical or forbidden practices. Characters Who Attempt to Replace the Emperor I hope the game allows players to enact scenarios similar to historical figures like: Dōkyō: A monk who tried to take the place of the Emperor during the Nara period, even being considered for imperial succession. Taira no Masakado: A rebellious noble who declared himself "Shinnō" (New Emperor) during the Heian period. These figures aimed to either directly claim the throne or establish themselves as divine rulers. Let players attempt such alternative paths to imperial power. Reviving the Missions to Tang China The Kentōshi or envoys to Tang China, were officially suspended in 894. I would like an option in the game to revisit this historical decision and choose whether or not to reinstate these diplomatic and cultural missions.

## Reply 105 — participant_077 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399581

There are a lot of interesting things in this diary, for a truly impressive ambition. I hope the best for it (and the inevitable performance challenge), but this here is a specific issue I'll raise as a concern I hope will be addressed in the future. I sincerely hope there are non-military ways for outsiders to hurt the legitimacy, or else this seems like a risk of hyper-stabilization. Raids are raids, of course, but the AI tends to defer to not attacking super-powerful empires unprovoked, unless the AI is irrational. And even when they do, they are more likely to lose. If either the AI or players are expected to beat down a stable-expansionist Chinese hegemon-title by... beating it militarily, that implies an expectation of hyper-military focus when (a) the AI to date can't even station MAA across the map effectively, and (b) would mean the player ends up with so much military that there are no other meaningful challenges. On one hand, I hope there are indirect military ways to degrade the hegemon. Things like the Iberian Struggle mechanics to sponsor peasant rebellions. Or the ability to help defend someone from a conquest, so you don't have to declare war / be declared upon. Or even a system to let outsiders 'join in' claimant factions, to try and throw some civil war risks. But what I really hope is that there are scheme-conflicts that outsiders can take against (or in) a hegemon, without taking away from the 1 personal / 1 hostile scheme for your 'normal' interactions. Call it a 'Hegemon scheme slot', representing how everyone in the region has to orient some thoughts regarding the Hegemon somehow. Schemes that let you use your Intrigue, or Learning, or Diplomacy, or Stewardship to degrade hegemonic legitimacy, without a direct war of aggression. The rest of this is just thoughts, but could be things like- -Military 'advisors' / MAA-loans to empire targets (to bolster their resilience) or powerful vassals / families in the hegemony, to increase civil war issues. AI pursues this based on perceived threat / alliances / etc. -Diplomatic support campaigns for claimants / rivals, 'boosting' a family's political influence, factional draw, or even willingness to create a claimant faction or scheme. AI pursues support for friends / allies, and against rivals. -Intrigue slander campaigns against court officials the sovereign has appointed, causing illegitimacy / backlash (because their character/merit is a reflection on the Emperor). AI pursues this in rivalry or hostility opinions. -Stewardship smuggling schemes, like smuggling silk worms, duplicating false artifacts, and some of those adventurer-themed criminal jobs. Bankroll the breakdown in trust in symbols of authority, or even outshine the Emperor with your own extravagence. AI pursues based on greed. -Learning schemes and challenges, similar to the 'condemn worldly vassal' or 'literalist debate' religious tenet options. Put your virtue / learning vis-a-vis the Emperor (or his chief advisors), and make the heavenly bureacracy look ill-informed and incompetent by comparison. AI pursues based on zealousness. Or activity efforts. -Hosting hedonistic feasts / seduction scheme against the Emperor's family members (or senior appointees to an effect decreasing by rank). Personal impropriety / hedonism for those led into temptation. -Hunts where at outrageous cost you invite and then outperform the Emperor. (Que an event where the emperor falls off the horse, and demands it to be struck from the record.) -Grand tournaments with a 'disgrace hegemon' intent, where every defeat of the hegemon's representatives works against the Hegemon's legitimacy / cultural acceptance of subjugated cultures. -'Hostile' legends, which create worse effects as they spread outside the empire (resistance to being tributary) and inside the empire.

## Reply 112 — participant_040 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399613

> Benismann said: I'll be very cynical right away and ask - is there no way to lose merit? Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
You can lose merit if you do things that are not becoming of your station. Generally its something that goes up as long as you are employed (but how quickly depends on what opportunities you get and how well you do). To reach the top you need quite a high score. Think of it as a virtual CV value, it is what you are rated on for new jobs and its a numerical value that shows how important the positions you held up until now were.

## Reply 114 — participant_082 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399619

Really hyped for All Under Heaven, the dynastic cycle and merit system look great. But if Paradox wants Tang China to feel right, they have to include the Jiedushi , the military governors who ran the northern frontier. These weren’t just bureaucrats. They were powerful warlords with their own armies, keeping out steppe threats like the Khitans and Turks. In return, they got autonomy. Sometimes they stayed loyal, sometimes they rebelled with An Lushan being the obvious example. That rebellion nearly collapsed the Tang and directly led to the Five Dynasties mess. Right now the system looks very court-focused. That’s fine for central China, but the north was a military zone. Youzhou, Hedong, etc. were practically independent states with nominal loyalty to the emperor. That tension between central control and frontier autonomy is a core part of Chinese history in this period. What I'd want:​ Governors on the frontier get special rights or mechanics with more troops, unique buildings, stronger forts. But the more powerful they get, the more likely they are to go rogue, especially during unstable/chaotic dynastic phases. Their loyalty should depend on stuff like: are they getting paid? Is the emperor dealing with disasters? Are the borders secure? It’s a perfect fit for CK3’s mechanics like western dukes going independent, but Chinese-style. Adds depth, history, and a reason to care about border provinces. Without something like this, the Tang collapse just won’t play out the way it should. Hope the devs consider it.

## Reply 158 — participant_063 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399820

Thanks for this DD, and I'm really excited about the upcoming expansion. In this post, I did want to put out a few concerns/ideas about the implementation of Japan. I like seeing how the design threads the needle of including the Fujiwara kampakus, the insei system of rule by retired emperors, and the rising shogunate, all while operating within the base CK3 design. I was thinking that Japan would be the preeminent place to flesh out the diarchy mechanics. it sounds like the system being implemented doesn't do that, but nonetheless is interesting and intricate. I do have a couple reservations and ideas though: 1. Shugo vs Soryo The DD lays out a two-fold division of vassal types for Japan, with Ritsuryo appointed civilian governors vs hereditary Soryo warriors. The soryo system isn't really a type of political office or power though. Rather, it's a term for the flexible inheritance practices that developed within warrior clans and is reflected in the Gosebei Shikimoku promulgated during the Kamakura period. So yes, warrior houses should have great flexibility in choosing the heirs of their households and that could be called Soryo succession. But, that's not the shogunate analog of the Ritsuryo governors - that should be the shugo post. When the Kamakura Shogunate took power, they appointed shugos to the various provinces. The term is translated either as "constable" or "military governor", and both terms are most appropriate for different time periods because the post started out as an official with circumscribed duties but eventually grew in power and importance and eclipsed the civilian Ritsuryo governors. Over time the shugo position became more hereditary and many shugo-holding clans eventually became the more well-known daimyo of the Warring States period, but this was a slide that happened due to weakness in the Shogunate and not by intent. So, what I would propose is that instead of Soryo vassals, there be Shugo vassals. They would similarly have a more military bent than the Ritsuryo governors, but at least at first they would be appointed. Then, the Shugo would be able to use various methods to convert their appointed holding into an inherited one. This is something that's already present in the Admin government in how you can petition the liege to make your governorship hereditary, but I'd recommend making it easier to pull off under a Shogunate and harder for the Shogun to reverse. 2. What happens to the kampaku and/or cloistered emperor after a shogunate arises? What I really like about studying the Kamakura period is how the institutions of the cloistered emperor and kampaku continued to be present, even if they were in a sometimes lopsided power sharing arrangement with the shogunate in Kamakura. There was always a possibility of reversing the situation. Retired emperor Go-Toba tried and failed to overthrow the shogunate in the 1221 Jokyu war, and Go-Daigo tried and was temporarily successful during the Kenmu Restoration of 1333-36. The Fujiwara regents were secondary players during the period, but you can imagine them as along for the ride in the event of a successful restoration of full civilian rule. What I'm concerned about is that it sounds like the Kampaku title disappears and is replaced by a Shogun as soon as a Shogun comes to power. (And since a cloistered emperor in-game depends on the emperor winning succession to the kampaku title, that would disappear as well.) Does that mean that there's sort of a one-way trip down the path to military rule if a Shogun is seated? I'd love to see continued interplay between the Shogun and the emperor and kampaku after that point, with the shogun needing to invest into keeping the Imperial household and Kyoto aristocracy in check to prevent their resurgence.

## Reply 177 — participant_112 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399883

Huge update! As a Southeast Asian and friend of the ROA team, my first thoughts are towards the Devaraja system. Overall the idea is amazing and the possible new gameplay loop is very interesting. It seems the current system are especially focused towards the Hindu and Buddhist ruler of the region. I have two big questions: How would the game handle the multifaith nature of beliefs in not just Southeast Asia but also Asia at large? Javanese text "Sutasoma" by the Buddhist Mpu Tantular explicitly mentioned the belief that Shiva and Buddha are two but one and the same 'truth', and there are temples in Java that features both Hindu and Buddhist style feature. Many even worship both as one Buddha Wiswa (Shiva-Buddha). Similarly in Japan, the worship of local Shinto Kami run alongside Buddhist practices and the difference between the two act more like a spectrum than two different faiths. I would love to see a new mechanic that can properly showcase these syncretism, especially in personal level and could possibly back-applied to other syncretic 'border' faiths like Mozarabism or the Conversos. What would happen when other faith spread to Southeast Asia, or if a player character change to Mandala system from another religion? Would an Asatru Malay Raja also be treated as Devaraja? What about historical albeit anachronistic Muslim rulers? I can see parallel system between the Devaraja and Caliphal title, where the Muslim Mataram Sultanate and its descendants in Yogyakarta and Surakarta claim power from their piety and justify their status as being granted on them by the Caliphs or Sunan. Arguably even the modern Yogyakarta Sultanate still practice this Devaraja aspect, with ties to very much unislamic deities like the Southern Sea Goddess and many other mystic ceremonies to enforce their legitimacy, and their subject to this day treat the Sultanate institutions as sacred. Lastly I want to comment that while temples indeed play crucial role in the region, it is because the temples are important part of their rituals and ceremonies, which imo should play larger role in maintaining their stature and legitimacy as a Mandala ruler. Maybe more pious activities should be tied to this? Another aspect that seems to be lacking is trade. While I understand introducing trade in the same update that expand the map might be too ambitious, I think a representation of it should exist in some way as maintaining a powerful control over the trades were the source of many regional power's reach and the reason why tributary missions to China are sent many times over the years.

## Reply 189 — participant_040 · 2025-05-27 · post-30399979

> Earendil Ardamire said: Will Chola and maybe the other Tamil states use the mandala government? Perhaps Sri Lanka also Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
The Mandala system does not really fit India as well as it does Southeast Asia. A loose network of tributaries and the claim to be a Devarajas/God Kings are not things you saw in India in the way you do in Southeast Asia at all. For now Feudal is a lot closer to what fits (though you may argue for multiple modes of government also inside India of course) since in many places there was often a strong focus on lineage, and on rulers granting lands to vassals that they then kept in perpituity. These land grants, which survive to this day, also often had the genealogies on them which further honestly fit with the whole "feudal" aspect to it. It is not perfect (especially in the south) but for now we have the preference to keep Mandalas for southeast asia, and leave India with Feudalism at least generally since the Mandala system the way we cover it is less fitting than what we have there now.

Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
I'M IN HEAVEN ALREADY JUST READING THIS >o< Ahem... okay, but before I let myself get completely lost to gushing, I have one concern that the diary made me worry about a little bit: ...the dreaded L-word @.@ I believe that Legitimacy is a non-mechanic, the way it's implemented and balanced (or rather, not balanced) right now. It ceases to be a concern the moment it's maxed out, and maxing it out is a matter of throwing a few parties/funerals. Managing Legitimacy currently boils down to spending a relatively small amount of an infinite resource (gold) just to get it out of our hair and forget it's there for a long time. So I am super hoping that the fact that you are tying such a mechanically interesting and historically flavourful mechanic to Legitimacy implies that you are looking at fundamentally overhauling Legitimacy. And... by that, I mean... ...and this may be an unpopular opinion, but... ...I think Legitimacy should be the Dark Souls of CK3's mechanics. It should never, for one minute, be taken for granted or possible to ignore by any character who is subject to it. The only escape from the trials and tribulations of Legitimacy, should be downgrading to a title tier that doesn't have it. Of course, it should be absolutely possible for a low-legitimacy ruler to get by, but only by making costly concessions to all the people keeping them in power. But if a ruler aspires to maintain high Legitimacy, they should be facing meaningful dilemmas and aggressive opposition (without and within) actively seeking to undermine it. The challenges the game throws at us should be scaled to the level of power it gives us. At least that's my 2 silvers. Whatever you do with Legitimacy, ultimately what I'm hoping for is that you balance it in such a way that the Dynastic Cycle will be able to... function properly, even when an experienced player is involved. Please don't make it cheap and cheesable. From the day CK3 was first announced, I have dreamt of the day China would become a part of it, and now that we are closer to that day than ever before, I dream that this expansion will be CK3's best foot forward yet. The potential of China is infinite; its history is full of drama, friction, intrigue. Cycles of ups and downs, big and small. Stories of rise to power and fall from grace. A discarded concubine could game the system to become the first and last Tianhou in her own right. An empire could go from standing at the very peak of prosperity to collapsing under its own weight. And CK3 is THE perfect game to experience all of that sweet, sweet, delicious drama in. It has the potential to unlock the full potential of China like no other Paradox game has done before. You just... need to allow yourselves to throw some uncompromising challenges at the players when it matters. And I think it matters now. TL;DR: If Legitimacy is such a big part of the Dynastic Cycle, please make it the most imaginably challenging and satisfying to overcome struggle that a Legitimacy mechanic can be.

## Reply 209 — participant_129 · 2025-05-27 · post-30400129

So please excuse the mechanical ramble, but one thing that just occurred to me that may be a use of the merit currency/system that's being talked about here that, while I fully admit a significant rework that probably isn't in the cards, might have some potential if there's an early enough pivot to at least make a really cool system possible in the future. My issue is that currently it looks a bit like a lightly relabeled legitimacy (same linear style) and tbh I've been disappointed by the lack of real impact of the legitimacy system in my games Here's my pitch to incorporate merit in a system that makes it matter for something other than "number goes up" (and maybe even hook it into the personality system): For a Celestial government, the three main aspects are merit, prestige, and piety (for the sake of argument for Admin you can replace merit with influence, and potentially Feudal w/ Legitimacy and Nomad w/ Herd). While those numbers are still used and spent as normal, the levels associated with them (for example, Prestige being broken down into Disgraced, Established, Distinguished, Illustrious, Exalted, Living Legend) give you a weight (potentially ticking) in that category towards one of those three aspects/categories. Which category? Stay with me here, my thinking is to make this less linear and more of a spectrum: Each character, based on their personality, traits, attributes, and life circumstances/events, is likely going to drift towards different parts of the specturm, that being prestige, piety, and merit. But it introduces a level of dynamism! Image a prestige-centered count being gung-ho about building up his army and helping in the coming crusade. He would probably pretty far on one corner of the triangle. But then, he goes crusading, becomes a holy warrior, maybe has an event that changes him from lustful to humble, and suddenly gets a lot of piety that skews his balance a bit, now he's a bit in the middle between the pious and prestigious. This is really more of a framework, that serves a similar purpose to the personality system, but I think it actually fits well as almost a mechanical-RP compliment (a zealous character is going to veer towards being on the pious side, being on the pious side should encourage them to do certain things like a buff to convert territories, better relations with other pious characters, worse relations with heretics or non-pious characters, etc). But I think something like that would be a way to unify the personality system with the mana/reputation system in a really satisfying mechanical way.

## Reply 220 — participant_134 · 2025-05-27 · post-30400232

I just want to convince you to make the Tiwi Islands playable. I believe that for the Paradox Interactive team integrating the Tiwi Islands as a fully playable region with its unique culture isn't just about "diversity." It's an opportunity to enrich the gameplay experience in Southeast Asia and offer players a truly fresh and intriguing challenge. Tiwi Islands have been geographically isolated from mainland Australia for millennia. This isolation led to the development of a language isolate, distinctive artistic styles (especially the burial poles and geometric patterns), and self-contained religious and ceremonial practices such as Pukumani and Kulama. In CK3, where every culture boasts its own traditions, aesthetics, and even unique buildings or decisions, Tiwi culture wouldn't just be "another one"—it would be genuinely distinctive. Imagine the possibilities of playing as a Tiwi ruler, nurturing these unique facets. This isn't just a stat boost; it's a deep dive into an unfamiliar world. You guys could add, for example, a "Pukumani Ceremony" tradition that enhances cultural acceptance or prestige; the ability to construct Tutini (burial poles), granting bonuses to piety, prestige, or even reducing opinion malus from different religions/cultures; a decision to "Foster Tiwi Art," unlocking bonuses to development growth or cultural opinion. The Tiwi people's fierce resistance, their history of successfully fending off Makassar traders, and even their staunch opposition to the British (Fort Dundas) make them ideal candidates for a culture with robust defensive traditions. Players choosing the Tiwi could receive bonuses when defending their home territory, making for a fascinating gameplay archetype: a small but incredibly resilient people. This could be reflected in unique Combat Traditions (e.g., "Bush Warriors" or "Island Defenders") that grant bonuses to defence in owned provinces or levy effectiveness on their home terrain. Tiwi matrilineal society is a goldmine for CK3, where marriage and inheritance mechanics are central. This could be implemented as a unique cultural tradition or even an inheritance law, where the primary heir is determined through the female line, or where the status of women in society is significantly higher than in most other cultures. This is a huge shift in player strategy for marriages and dynasty building. The Tiwi could start with a tribal government type that truly reflects their decentralized structure. Instead of strict hereditary monarchies, leadership could transition to respected elders or the most prestigious/knowledgeable clan members. This could be represented by unique succession laws or even cultural traditions that empower a "Council of Elders" with significant influence over key decisions. Legendary figures like Mudangkala, the blind old woman creator (Wow!), could be integrated into their religion or culture, offering unique events or bonuses tied to ancestral worship or creation myths. Instead of leaving the Tiwi Islands as merely "unknown land," they could become a dynamic gateway to the region. Players exploring the southeastern seas might encounter unexpectedly strong and unique resistance, creating engaging stories of first contact. The presence of a culture as distinct and isolated as the Tiwi would create an interesting contrast with the more developed or expansive empires of Southeast Asia. This opens up new avenues for cultural interaction, conflict, and unique story arcs as players (or AI) from other cultures attempt to engage with the Tiwi. Many CK3 players, including me, appreciate deep historical and cultural nuances. Tiwi culture, with its rich history and unique traditions, offers a fresh experience that stands apart from the usual European or Asian cultures. This is truly an Australian culture! This will appeal to players looking for something genuinely new and exotic.

I'm thinking in particular of the Southern Song era, when the Jurchen Jin dynasty was able to conquer the north but not the south, and China ended up with a fairly stable north/south political divide until the Mongol conquests. Thirdly, could this expansion be the ideal opportunity to introduce councilor mechanics? After all, the most senior, prestigious and influential roles in the Chinese state were generally the heads of government departments located in the capital, not the regional governors located in the provinces. > Princess Stabbity said: I'M IN HEAVEN ALREADY JUST READING THIS >o< Ahem... okay, but before I let myself get completely lost to gushing, I have one concern that the diary made me worry about a little bit: ...the dreaded L-word @.@ I believe that Legitimacy is a non-mechanic, the way it's implemented and balanced (or rather, not balanced) right now. It ceases to be a concern the moment it's maxed out, and maxing it out is a matter of throwing a few parties/funerals. Managing Legitimacy is currently just a matter of spending a relatively small amount an infinite resource (gold) just to get it out of our hair and forget it's there for a long time. So I am super hoping that the fact that you are tying such a mechanically interesting and historically flavourful mechanic to Legitimacy implies that you are looking at fundamentally overhauling Legitimacy. And... by that, I mean... ...and this may be an unpopular opinion, but... ...I think Legitimacy should be the Dark Souls of CK3's mechanics. It should never, for one minute, be taken for granted or possible to ignore by any character who is subject to it. The only escape from the trials of tribulations of Legitimacy, should be downgrading to a title tier that doesn't have it. It should be absolutely possible for a low-legitimacy ruler to get by, but only making costly concessions to all the people keeping them in power. And if a ruler aspires to maintain high Legitimacy, they should be facing meaningful dilemmas and aggressive opposition (without and within) actively seeking to undermine it. At least that's my 2 silvers. Whatever you do with Legitimacy, ultimately the end goal that I'm hoping for is that you balance it in such a way that the Dynastic Cycle will be able to... function properly, even when an experienced player is involved. Please don't make it cheap and cheesable. From the day CK3 was first announced, I have dreamt of the day China would become a part of it, and now that we are closer to that day than ever before, I dream that this expansion will be CK3's best foot forward yet. The potential of China is infinite; its history is full of drama, friction, intrigue. Cycles of ups and downs, big and small. Stories of rise to power and fall from grace. A discarded concubine could game the system to become the first and last Tianhou in her own right. An empire could go from standing at the very peak of prosperity to collapsing under its own weight. And CK3 is THE perfect game to experience all of that sweet, sweet, delicious drama in. It has the potential to unlock the full potential of China like no other Paradox game has done before. You just... need to allow yourselves to throw some uncompromising challenges at the players when it matters. And I think it matters now. TL;DR: If Legitimacy is such a big part of the Dynastic Cycle, please make it the most imaginably challenging and satisfying to overcome that a Legitimacy mechanic can be. Click to expand...

Also, the tendency of ambitious Chinese men to increase their perceived merit by withdrawing to the mountains - thus demonstrating their moral elevation and lack of concern for political power - whilst making sure that the mountains they withdrew to were as close to the court as possible, so that their noble lack of worldly ambition would be noticed by the emperor and it would be as straightforward as possible to reward their virtue with higher office, was apparently so common it became a medieval Chinese meme. https://en.clearharmony.net/articles/a21952-The-Chinese-Idiom-of-Zhongnan-Mountain-Shortcut.html

## Reply 252 — participant_156 · 2025-05-27 · post-30400543

I love the addition of the Hegemony tier and that it is pretty restricted, but I feel like it represents what "Empire" tier titles should be. Right now, they're just big kingdoms in the majority of cases, and there's not even any justification for any of them besides the HRE and ERE for even calling themselves "empires" and especially not their rulers "emperors" as in medieval Europe that term pretty much exclusively referred to The Empire, Rome. I think Hegemony and Empire tiers should have their terminology swapped. Have the currently existing de jure Empire titles be Hegemonies, their rulers stay Kings (possibly High Kings? Maybe just in certain cases), have feudal king-tier vassals of Hegemonies be Princes or Grand Dukes or something to that effect, similarly to how Petty King dukes become proper Dukes when under a Kingdom, and special cases like the HRE, ERE and Japan can keep "empire" in the name because that is what they were actually referred to, the former two because they claimed the legacy of Rome. And when you actually restore Rome, now you are a genuine, bona fide Emperor ruling a proper Empire. If nothing else, at least have it as an optional game rule. This feels like a simple change to implement that would go a long way to giving the term "Empire" in the game much more weight than it does now and have it used correctly.

> Trin Tragula said: House Relations & Blocs​The long-term relationships between different Houses are now tracked, and move along a sliding scale from great friendship to intense rivalry. Whenever a member of a House takes a significant friendly or hostile action against another House’s member, such as a marriage or a murder, the relation between the Houses will change to reflect the act. View attachment 1303505 In Japan, houses can also form Blocs. These are groups of houses that all follow the head of one house, largely based on house relations. A bloc is a military alliance that is represented on the map, similar to steppe confederations, though with a clear leader and more far-reaching responsibilities. Bloc members will join the factions and wars of their leader (offensive as well as defensive). This means that when wars break out in Japan they are not as limited despite the fragmented political situation. Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
There is a number of mechanics in this expansion that could liven up the map in other areas. Is there any chance house relations and blocs could be extended to other governments?

## Reply 279 — participant_176 · 2025-05-27 · post-30400949

Great work! I have a few thoughts about Chinese Central Bureaucracy Titles Without Actual Land: In stable periods of ancient China, there was a distinct separation between central government officials and local governors. When a local governor was promoted to serve in the central government (e.g., as Minister of Revenue or Personnel), they typically relinquished their local titles. These central posts, while not tied to any land, often held greater political power and were considered prestigious promotions. Would it be possible to reflect this system in-game by allowing characters to hold powerful non-landed central offices that supersede local titles? For example, the minister of personnel can have the advantages in determining the appointment of the local governors. Imperial Examinations for Early Career Officers: In historical practice, high-ranking officials did not re-enter the imperial examination system, as it was primarily designed to select entry-level bureaucrats. I wonder if the game would limit exam access based on current rank or career stage to reflect this. Civil-Military Relationship and Economic Dependency: In many dynasties, military officers were reliant on the central or civil administration for funding, rather than controlling their own local tax base. This allowed the central government to maintain indirect control over regional military forces. Could the game model this relationship—perhaps by linking military governors to economic support directly from the central treasury rather than local income?

"The Silk Road​While China was enormously important in East Asia and its surroundings, for many parts of the world China’s impact was more indirect. IMHO it would be sensible if that was an Alexander-theme empire. Shahnama has Iskandar as the son of Persian king and a Macedonian princess who recovered his birthright!, and generally later Persian and Islamic traditions are very approving of Alexander. Heck, Timur, and Mughals both kinda used his legitimacy through their usage of Sahib-e-Qiran-e-Sani (Second Lord of Auspicious Conjuction) title. (The title itself is basically someone born under specific planetary conjunction – but the first one to add such title was Alexander himself.) so… while Persian Empires are great, I just think that Alexander the Great-themed Empire would be the best, because it makes sense for just about everyone. I mean: you have a Persian Empire that grew too big? Check, it fits! You have a Crusader conquering Persian Empire? Check, it fits! You have a Mashriqi ruler who went east? Great, it fits!

## Reply 310 — participant_194 · 2025-05-28 · post-30401391

## Reply 311 — participant_195 · 2025-05-28 · post-30401392

What I'm most impressed is with the sheer amount of content that is being hinted at here, it seems like the AUH update will add an order of magnitude more content to the game than Roads to Power did, and that one was already very ambitious in comparison to previous updates. I have the impression that the first 5 years of CK3 development were primarily dedicated to clearing the way for this update and getting past every bottleneck that was on the way. Some comments and suggestions: The basic presentation of the Celestial government is looking pretty great. Many people are arguing that the Imperial Treasury mechanic should also be added to the Byzantine-styled administrative government and I completely agree with them. The Imperial Treasury mechanic seems pretty good and describes the way the Byzantine government operated historically better than the current system and would improve it. Please, add that mechanic to the general Admin government! And still speaking of the Byzantine government, one common (and in my opinion, correct) criticism I've seen of it is that there is no way to lose your Governorship if you perform badly as a Governor. While we'll need more time to see further details on the Merit system, I do think something like it should very well be adapted to the Byzantine government - the Roman Emperor valued Merit in choosing appointments very much, this isn't an exclusively Confucian worry! Of course it would have to operate differently (the Byzantines did not have an Imperial Examination nor the broadly Confucian ideology that influenced Chinese notions of civil service), but I do think a mechanic that aims to represent meritocracy and that punished unworthy administrators that build a bad CV for themselves would be right at home in many other government types. In general, I think it's a bad idea to make certain mechanics too exclusive to certain regions when they represent features of human society that are very much universal. Every mechanic should initially apply to every place where it makes sense for it to belong, with custom-made variations of that mechanic being placed in different regions that had specific cultural variations of that phenomenon. I already think that some mechanics that are exclusive to Administrative Government (such as Realm Governance Issues, or certain Political Schemes such as Slander) are something that should exist in some form in all other Realms, so I don't want to see Celestial Governments doubling down on this. Besides this, the one thing about the Celestial Government that I really have mixed feelings about is the implementation of the Dynastic Cycle. For me, one of the best features of CK3 is how much of the game's simulation organically emerges from the operation of many moving parts that each represent a relatively concrete social phenomena, instead of having mechanics that operate at a higher level of abstraction and railroad events more - for instance, the game simulates the opinion that every vassal has of you and that opinion (among other factors) determine whether they will want to rebel. This makes the game far more immersive, engaging and fun to toy with than if it had something like a "Realm Stability" Bar that automatically triggers a rebellion with X number of vassals if it falls below a threshold. However I feel that some mechanics released in recent times (first House Unity, then Legitimacy...) have been moving against that particular design principle and have leaned towards more and more abstraction, and Dynastic Cycle seems to be another greater step in that direction. I imagine there are many good reasons why it's necessary to have a more abstract mechanic to represent something as complex as the Chinese concept of the Dynastic Cycle (no doubt, trying to make a rise-and-fall, unite-and-divide cycle that emerges organically from an in-depth simulation would be extremely difficult to program and balance and would fry a few CPU's), and a turn towards more abstraction was likely inevitable. But I do strongly hope that you ensure the Dynastic Cycle system is detailed, immersive and doesn't feel railroaded, and that you will prefer leaning towards organic simulations over more abstracted mechanics whenever it is feasible to do so. It's unclear how much of what was presented here will be on the free update and how much will be DLC exclusive. As you know, making the entirety of the map expansion and all new governments DLC-exclusive would make it much harder to make the features of these unique governments interact with new mechanics from future DLC and will thus limit how much you can build upon them. I already think it's a shame that Landless is exclusive to RtP (I believe the landless system has much potential for future development and refinement and interacting with future game mechanics, but being a DLC-exclusive makes that much harder) and I hope AUH doesn't do the same on a far greater magnitude. I'm looking forward to seeing how Culture and Religion will operate in East Asia. Religion in Japan in the game's time period was heavily syncretic, to the point there was no real demarcation between Shinto and Buddhism - Buddhist temples had shrines to the Kamis, the population mostly practiced Buddhism and revered the Kamis on the same breath, the Emperor claimed descent from Amaterasu and was also a practicing Buddhist... It's unclear how the current Religion mechanics are going to deal with this. The way the multiple Chinese Schools of Thought operated is also extremely important - Confucianism and Daoism were at the same time distinct schools of theology/ritual/esoteric practices within the broader Chinese folk religion and also political philosophies and scholarly traditions in their own right and also had a complicated relationship with Chinese Buddhism. I know nothing about religion in medieval Korea but I imagine it too must have been a complex mix of previous folk traditions, Chinese philosophical influences and the local variants of Buddhism.

## Reply 313 — participant_090 · 2025-05-28 · post-30401421

<!-- artifact:quote:start -->
> Trin Tragula said: The Mandala system does not really fit India as well as it does Southeast Asia. A loose network of tributaries and the claim to be a Devarajas/God Kings are not things you saw in India in the way you do in Southeast Asia at all. For now Feudal is a lot closer to what fits (though you may argue for multiple modes of government also inside India of course) since in many places there was often a strong focus on lineage, and on rulers granting lands to vassals that they then kept in perpituity. These land grants, which survive to this day, also often had the genealogies on them which further honestly fit with the whole "feudal" aspect to it. It is not perfect (especially in the south) but for now we have the preference to keep Mandalas for southeast asia, and leave India with Feudalism at least generally since the Mandala system the way we cover it is less fitting than what we have there now. Click to expand...

Click to expand...
<!-- artifact:quote:end -->
To add to this Manichaeism (known as Monijiao in China) of all things had a huge affect on Chinese political and religious philosophy at this time, it was incredibly popular and provoked several persecutions and despite centuries of persecution Monijiao became a hugely influential on Chinese folk religion and Chinese Buddhism to the point there are a handful of intact Manichean temples in China that survived masquerading as temples to "the Buddha of light" a euphemism for Mani. It only really ceased to be a practiced religion in the 14th-15th century when it merged with Chinese Buddhism after centuries of syncreticism. There are even debatable tales of extant Manichean practices preserved as folk rituals in rural China today. Wikipedia has a decent page on the subject for anyone interested.

## Reply 316 — participant_197 · 2025-05-28 · post-30401466

One mistake about the de jure Hegemony map of China is that the Southwest of China including modern Yunnan and most of Guizhou should not be part of China's Hegemony de jure during CK3's timeframe. The Southwest of China was in fact independent as Nanzhao during Tang's period and Dali during Song's period. Tang and Song even waged war against them several times but never succeed in annexing or subjugating the Southwest until Yuan Mongols did. Even after Yuan, China did not really established a direct control over the Southwest until the "Gaituguiliu" policy promoted in late Ming and Qing period.

It's a point I made in another thread before it got closed down there. But there's a lot of carry over between the Byzantine admin and Chinese celestial government so far. How do the devs draw the line between what is unique to the Byzantines and what is unique to the Chinese is somewhat complicated. A few things that are unique to Byzantines is they tend to have powerful generals becoming emperors or co-emperors, and power and wars quickly flow outside of the dynasty country as you get ambitious leaders toppling new dynasty after new dynasties. But in terms of actually running the empire, a lot of operating system remains the same, because on many levels there are only so many differences in terms of bureaucratic functions. Byzantines do draw more from a military-class than some Chinese dynasties did to become governors, as the Song dynasty tried its best to separate civil office from military offices.

## Reply 331 — participant_090 · 2025-05-28 · post-30401583

Id actually like to formally make the argument that Persia/Eran/Iran should be a formable Hegemon, in that it shares a lot of traits with both Rome and China. for a start It is the earliest persistently reforming Empire. Coalescing under Cyrus the great in 550bc, The Qin first unified an imperial Chinese state in 221bc and Rome depending on how you want to go about defining "empire" in its context is debatably anywhere between 201bc with the end of the second Punic war confirming Rome as the undisputed superpower of the Mediterranean to the ascension of Augustus to Emperor in 27bc. Its also remarkably culturally resilient like China being invaded and toppled many more times than China yet staying culturally homogeneous and various native polities and dynasties rising up to varying success to reestablish native rule to Persia. Lastly many of its dynasties and empires, Persian or foreign have engaged in outward conquest of areas outside of the Persian core area the Archamenids are arguably the first hegemonic empire as we would understand it. In the wake of Alexander, Bactria Invaded and conqured large portions of northern India. Another example is the imperial capitol of the Parthian and Sassanid empires, Ctesiphon, arguably an analogous forerunner to today's Baghdad. Outside what is traditionally considered Persia proper. The Selujk invasion of Anatolia debatably, depending on how you'd define them. I cant see a way you can define Hegemon to include Rome, Mongolia and China but not Persia.
